The original Moskva (Russian: ÐœÐ¾ÑÐºÐ²Ð°Ì) was one of six Leningrad-class destroyer leaders built for the Soviet Navy during the 1930s. It was completed in 1938 and assigned to the Black Sea Fleet.  She participated in the Raid on ConstanÈ›a.  On 26 June 1941, a few days after the beginning of the German invasion of the Soviet Union, she struck a mine and was sunk. The guided missile cruiser Moskva was launched as the Slava on 1979.  She was re-fitted and re-commissioned as the Moskov in 2000.  The two "Moskov's" are now less than 100 miles apart, on the seabed of the Black Sea.

The original Moskva (Russian: ÐœÐ¾ÑÐºÐ²Ð°Ì) was one of six Leningrad-class destroyer leaders built for the Soviet Navy during the 1930s. It was completed in 1938 and assigned to the Black Sea Fleet.  She participated in the Raid on ConstanÈ›a.  On 26 June 1941, a few days after the beginning of the German invasion of the Soviet Union, she struck a mine and was sunk. The guided missile cruiser Moskva was launched as the Slava on 1979.  She was re-fitted and re-commissioned as the Moskov in 2000.  The two "Moskov's" are now less than 100 miles apart, on the seabed of the Black Sea.

It's all well and good for a bunch of adventure-tourists with press vests to go around ruined cityscapes bothering people who have lost everything to ask them how they feel, but this is a far cry from being a true war correspondent.   The reason the actual military situation of this war is being so negligently and ignorantly reported on is that there appear to be no actual war correspondents with the nerve or determination to actually accompany combat units at length, not even to mention that none of these people ever seems to have learned even the most basic tenets of tactics, strategy, weaponry, battlefield terminology or military affairs in general.   They report everything like one great big human interest story, tailored to what they must assume is an audience as fourth-grade ignorant as the reporters are.  And yes, to their credit, it is all very human, and very interesting, but it is not war reporting. It is civilian-tragedy reporting, and for what it is, some of it has been exemplary.   But we are seeing the results of a campus-liberal intelligentsia assigned by history the honor and privilege of reporting on the most crucial events in human history since the Cuban missile crisis, and all they see is a big human interest story. The military stuff, that's just sooooo icky. Leave it to the retired generals and think-tankers back home to try and make sense of the actual warfare going on, even if what they are given from colleagues in the field is so bereft of military information that all the analysts can do is say 'probably' a lot along with various partisan opinions about all things Kremlin.  The most technologically-capable status of world news reporting in known history has met the biggest story of anyone's lifetime, and they just have no idea how to go about reporting on it. The press performance in this war ought to be remembered by history as the furthest the trade of journalism has ever descended into narrative-pitching guesswork and endless editorializing based on hardly any real facts at all. And maybe, at long last, the profession will remember what its ONLY job is:  TO REPORT FACTS.

Soviet/ Russian warships have a couple of major design flaws.   One is that it is standard practice on Soviet/Russian warships to NOT have their firefighting systems energized for instantaneous use in dealing with fires.   The VMF lost a destroyer n the 1970s due to this fault.   The other major design flaw is the vulnerability of their missile loadout to battle damage.   The antiship missiles and the antiaircraft missiles are highly vulnerable if the warship is struck by missiles, bombs, and gunfire.   This is a lesson which the VMF has not learned yet.  It seems likely that another flaw exists in damage control concerning a flooding casualty.   If the shipâ€™s pumps are dependent upon an auxiliary power source then the clear indication is that the pumps did not have sufficient power to operate and de-water the ship.   De-watering the ship would be necessary since water is used to suppress any fires in belowdecks spaces.   Additionally, the ship has an odd arrangement of watertight compartments.
